<p><b>Elias James "E.J." Corey</b> (born July 12, 1928) is an American <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic chemist</a>. In 1990, he won the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> "for his development of the theory and methodology of <a href="/wiki/Organic_synthesis" title="Organic synthesis">organic synthesis</a>",<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> specifically <a href="/wiki/Retrosynthetic_analysis" title="Retrosynthetic analysis">retrosynthetic analysis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nobel-lecture_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobel-lecture-7">[7]</a></sup> Regarded by many as one of the greatest living chemists, he has developed numerous synthetic <a href="/wiki/Reagents" class="mw-redirect" title="Reagents">reagents</a>, methodologies and total syntheses and has advanced the science of organic synthesis considerably. </p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elias_James_Corey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>E.J. Corey (the surname was anglicized from the <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanese</a> Arabic <i><a href="/wiki/Khoury" class="mw-redirect" title="Khoury">Khoury</a></i>, meaning <i>priest</i>) was born to <a href="/wiki/Arab_Christians" title="Arab Christians">Christian</a> Lebanese immigrants in <a href="/wiki/Methuen,_Massachusetts" title="Methuen, Massachusetts">Methuen, Massachusetts</a>, 50&#160;km (31&#160;mi) north of Boston.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> His mother changed his name to "Elias" to honor his father, who died eighteen months after Corey's birth. His widowed mother, brother, two sisters and an aunt and uncle all lived together in a spacious house, struggling through the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>. As a young boy, Corey was rather independent and enjoyed sports such as baseball, football, and hiking. He attended a Catholic elementary school and <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_High_School_(Massachusetts)" title="Lawrence High School (Massachusetts)">Lawrence High School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lawrence,_Massachusetts" title="Lawrence, Massachusetts">Lawrence, Massachusetts</a>.</p>
<p>At the age of 16, Corey entered <a href="/wiki/MIT" class="mw-redirect" title="MIT">MIT</a>, where he earned both a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor%27s_degree" title="Bachelor's degree">bachelor's degree</a> in 1948 and a <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Philosophy" title="Doctor of Philosophy">Ph.D.</a> under Professor <a href="/wiki/John_C._Sheehan" title="John C. Sheehan">John C. Sheehan</a> in 1951. Upon entering MIT, Corey's only experience with science was in mathematics, and he began his college career pursuing a degree in engineering. After his first chemistry class in his sophomore year he began rethinking his long-term career plans and graduated with a bachelor's degree in chemistry. Immediately thereafter, at the invitation of Professor John C. Sheehan, Corey remained at MIT for his Ph.D. After his graduate career he was offered an appointment at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Illinois_at_Urbana%E2%80%93Champaign" title="University of Illinois at Urbana–Champaign">University of Illinois at Urbana–Champaign</a>, where he became a full professor of chemistry in 1956 at the age of 27. He was initiated as a member of the Zeta Chapter of <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Chi_Sigma" title="Alpha Chi Sigma">Alpha Chi Sigma</a> at the University of Illinois in 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-alphachisigma.org_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alphachisigma.org-9">[9]</a></sup> In 1959, he moved to <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>, where he is currently an emeritus professor of organic chemistry with an active Corey Group research program. He chose to work in organic chemistry because of <i>"</i>its intrinsic beauty and its great relevance to human health".<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> He has also been an advisor to Pfizer for more than 50 years.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among numerous honors, Corey was awarded the <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Science" title="National Medal of Science">National Medal of Science</a> in 1998,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> in 1990,<sup id="cite_ref-nobel-lecture_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobel-lecture-7">[7]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">American Chemical Society</a>'s greatest honor, the <a href="/wiki/Priestley_Medal" title="Priestley Medal">Priestley Medal</a>, in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-major-awards_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-major-awards-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_contributions">Major contributions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elias_James_Corey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Major contributions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reagents">Reagents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elias_James_Corey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Reagents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>E.J. Corey has developed several new synthetic reagents:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pyridinium_chlorochromate" title="Pyridinium chlorochromate">PCC (pyridinium chlorochromate)</a>, also referred to as the <b>Corey-Suggs reagent</b>, is widely used for the oxidation of <a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohols</a> to corresponding <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketones</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aldehyde" title="Aldehyde">aldehydes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> PCC has several advantages over other commercial oxidants.</li>
</ul>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:PCC_mechanism.png" class="image"><img alt="PCC mechanism.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/PCC_mechanism.png/700px-PCC_mechanism.png" width="700" height="155" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/PCC_mechanism.png/1050px-PCC_mechanism.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/PCC_mechanism.png/1400px-PCC_mechanism.png 2x" data-file-width="2194" data-file-height="485" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>One of these advantages is that the compound is available as an air-stable yellow solid that is not very hygroscopic. Unlike other oxidizing agents, PCC can accomplish single oxidations with only about 1.5 equivalents (scheme 1). The alcohol performs nucleophilic attack to the electropositive <a href="/wiki/Chromium" title="Chromium">chromium</a> (VI) metal displacing chlorine. The <a href="/wiki/Chloride" title="Chloride">chloride</a> <a href="/wiki/Anion" class="mw-redirect" title="Anion">anion</a> then acts as a base to afford the aldehyde product and chromium (IV). The slightly acidic character of PCC makes it useful for cyclization reactions with alcohols and <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkenes</a> (Scheme 2).<sup id="cite_ref-acidic_chromium_oxidation_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acidic_chromium_oxidation-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:PCC_under_acidic_conditions2.png" class="image" title="reactivity of PCC under acidic conditions"><img alt="reactivity of PCC under acidic conditions" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/PCC_under_acidic_conditions2.png/700px-PCC_under_acidic_conditions2.png" width="700" height="134" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/PCC_under_acidic_conditions2.png/1050px-PCC_under_acidic_conditions2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/PCC_under_acidic_conditions2.png/1400px-PCC_under_acidic_conditions2.png 2x" data-file-width="2016" data-file-height="385" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>The initial oxidation yields the corresponding aldehyde, which can then undergo a <a href="/wiki/Prins_reaction" title="Prins reaction">Prins reaction</a> with the neighboring <a href="/wiki/Alkene" title="Alkene">alkene</a>. After elimination and further oxidation, the product is a cyclic <a href="/wiki/Ketone" title="Ketone">ketone</a>. If this product is undesired, powdered <a href="/wiki/Sodium_acetate" title="Sodium acetate">sodium acetate</a> can be used as a buffer to achieve only initial oxidation. The robustness of PCC as an oxidizing agent has also rendered it useful in the realm of total synthesis (Scheme 3). This example illustrates that PCC is capable of performing a <i>Dauben oxidative rearrangement</i> with tertiary alcohols through a [3,3]-sigmatropic rearrangement.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:PCC_rearrangement3.png" class="image" title="[3,3] rearrangement with PCC"><img alt="[3,3] rearrangement with PCC" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/PCC_rearrangement3.png/700px-PCC_rearrangement3.png" width="700" height="146" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/PCC_rearrangement3.png/1050px-PCC_rearrangement3.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/PCC_rearrangement3.png/1400px-PCC_rearrangement3.png 2x" data-file-width="2142" data-file-height="446" /></a></div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><i>t</i>-Butyldimethylsilyl ether (TBS),<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> triisopropylsilyl ether (TIPS), and methoxyethoxymethyl (MEM): are popular alcohol <a href="/wiki/Protecting_group" title="Protecting group">protecting groups</a>. The development of these protecting groups allowed the synthesis of several <a href="/wiki/Natural_product" title="Natural product">natural products</a> that did not have the <a href="/wiki/Functional_group" title="Functional group">functional group</a> compatibility to withstand standard chemical transformations. Although the synthetic community is now moving away from the use of protecting groups it is still rare that a published synthesis of a natural product omits them. Since 1972 the TBS group has become the most popular <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a> protecting group. TBS is stable to <a href="/wiki/Chromatography" title="Chromatography">chromatography</a> and labile enough for cleavage under basic and acidic conditions. More importantly, TBS <a href="/wiki/Ether" title="Ether">ethers</a> are stable to some carbon nucleophiles such as Grignard reagents and enolates.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:TBS_primary_deprotection4.png" class="image"><img alt="TBS primary deprotection4.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/TBS_primary_deprotection4.png/500px-TBS_primary_deprotection4.png" width="500" height="155" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/TBS_primary_deprotection4.png/750px-TBS_primary_deprotection4.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/TBS_primary_deprotection4.png/1000px-TBS_primary_deprotection4.png 2x" data-file-width="1679" data-file-height="520" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>In the field of complex molecule synthesis, TBS has been widely used as one of the most versatile of the silicon-based protecting groups (scheme 4).<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> The use of CSA provides selective removal of a primary TBS ether in the presence of tertiary TBS ether and TIPS ethers. Other means of TBS deprotection include acids (also Lewis acids), and <a href="/wiki/Fluoride" title="Fluoride">fluorides</a>. TIPS protecting groups were also pioneered by Corey and provide increased selectivity of primary alcohol protection over secondary and tertiary alcohol protection. TIPS ethers are more stable under acidic and basic conditions, the disadvantage of this protecting group over TBS ethers being that the group is less labile for deprotection.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The most common reagents used for cleavage employ the same conditions as TBS ether, but longer reaction times are generally needed.</p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Primary_TIPS_deprotection5.png" class="image"><img alt="Primary TIPS deprotection5.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Primary_TIPS_deprotection5.png/550px-Primary_TIPS_deprotection5.png" width="550" height="106" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Primary_TIPS_deprotection5.png/825px-Primary_TIPS_deprotection5.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Primary_TIPS_deprotection5.png/1100px-Primary_TIPS_deprotection5.png 2x" data-file-width="2033" data-file-height="391" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>Usually TBS ethers are severed by TBAF, but the hindered TBS ether above survives the reaction conditions upon primary TIPS removal (scheme 5).<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The MEM protecting group was first described by Corey in 1976.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> This protecting group is similar in reactivity and stability to other alkoxy methyl ethers under acidic conditions. Cleavage of MEM protecting groups is usually accomplished under acidic conditions, but coordination with metal halides greatly enhances lability via assisted cleavage (scheme 6).<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:MEM_Zn_deprotection6.png" class="image"><img alt="MEM Zn deprotection6.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/MEM_Zn_deprotection6.png/600px-MEM_Zn_deprotection6.png" width="600" height="146" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/MEM_Zn_deprotection6.png/900px-MEM_Zn_deprotection6.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/MEM_Zn_deprotection6.png/1200px-MEM_Zn_deprotection6.png 2x" data-file-width="2219" data-file-height="540" /></a></div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>1,3-<a href="/wiki/Dithiane" title="Dithiane">Dithianes</a> were pioneered by E.J. Corey in 1965 as a temporary modification of a <a href="/wiki/Carbonyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbonyl">carbonyl</a> group in displacement and addition reactions. The formation of dithiane was the initial development that introduced <a href="/wiki/Umpolung" title="Umpolung">Umpolung</a> chemistry and is widely used for inversion of reactivity. The formations of dithianes can be accomplished with a Lewis acid (scheme 7) or directly from carbonyl compounds.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Dithiane_formation7.png" class="image"><img alt="Dithiane formation7.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Dithiane_formation7.png/350px-Dithiane_formation7.png" width="350" height="79" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Dithiane_formation7.png/525px-Dithiane_formation7.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Dithiane_formation7.png/700px-Dithiane_formation7.png 2x" data-file-width="1152" data-file-height="260" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>The pKa of dithianes is approximately 30, allowing deprotonation with an alkyl lithium reagent, typically <a href="/wiki/N-butyllithium" class="mw-redirect" title="N-butyllithium">n-butyllithium</a>. The reaction with dithianes and aldehydes is now known as the <a href="/wiki/Corey-Seebach_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Corey-Seebach reaction">Corey-Seebach reaction</a>. The dithiane once deprotonated serves as an acyl anion used to attack incoming <a href="/wiki/Electrophile" title="Electrophile">electrophiles</a>. After deprotection of the dithiane, usually with HgO, a ketone product is observed from the masked acyl dithiane anion. The utility of such reactions has expanded the field of organic synthesis by allowing synthetic chemists to use Umpolung disconnections in total synthesis (scheme 8).<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> 1,3-dithianes are also used as protecting groups for carbonyl compounds expressing the versatility and utility of this functional group.</p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:1,2-dithiane_addition8.png" class="image"><img alt="1,2-dithiane addition8.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/1%2C2-dithiane_addition8.png/650px-1%2C2-dithiane_addition8.png" width="650" height="96" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/1%2C2-dithiane_addition8.png/975px-1%2C2-dithiane_addition8.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/1%2C2-dithiane_addition8.png/1300px-1%2C2-dithiane_addition8.png 2x" data-file-width="2270" data-file-height="336" /></a></div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>In addition, Corey commenced detailed studies on cationic polyolefin cyclizations utilized in enzymatic production of <a href="/wiki/Cholesterol" title="Cholesterol">cholesterol</a> from simpler plant terpenes.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Corey established the details of the remarkable cylization process by first studying the biological synthesis of sterols from squalene.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Methodology">Methodology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elias_James_Corey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Methodology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several reactions developed in Corey's lab have become commonplace in modern synthetic organic chemistry. At least 302 methods have been developed in the Corey group since 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Several reactions have been named after him:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Corey-Itsuno_reduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Corey-Itsuno reduction">Corey-Itsuno reduction</a>, also known as the Corey-Bakshi-Shibata reduction, is an enantioselctive reduction of ketones to alcohols utilizing an oxazaborolidine <a href="/wiki/Catalysis" title="Catalysis">catalyst</a> and various boranes as the stoichiometric reductant.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The Corey group first demonstrated the synthesis of the catalyst using borane and chiral aminoalcohols. The reaction uses the chiral amino acid <a href="/wiki/Proline" title="Proline">proline</a> and in the presence of borane leads to the <a href="/wiki/CBS_catalyst" title="CBS catalyst">CBS catalyst</a> (scheme 9).<sup id="cite_ref-reactions_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reactions-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-enantioselective_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enantioselective-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:CBS_formation9.png" class="image"><img alt="CBS formation9.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/CBS_formation9.png/600px-CBS_formation9.png" width="600" height="115" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/CBS_formation9.png/900px-CBS_formation9.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/CBS_formation9.png/1200px-CBS_formation9.png 2x" data-file-width="2016" data-file-height="386" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>Later, Corey demonstrated that substituted boranes were easier to prepare and much more stable. The reduction mechanism begins with the oxazoborolidine being only slightly basic at [nitrogen], coordinating with the stoichiometric borane of the boron amine complex(scheme 10).<sup id="cite_ref-enantioselective_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enantioselective-33">[33]</a></sup> Lack of donation from the nitrogen to the boron increases its Lewis acidity, allowing coordination with the ketone substrate. The complexation of the substrate occurs from the most accessible lone pair of the <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> leading to restricted rotation around the B-O bond due to the sterically neighboring phenyl group.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:CBS_mechanism10.png" class="image"><img alt="CBS mechanism10.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/CBS_mechanism10.png/400px-CBS_mechanism10.png" width="400" height="341" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/CBS_mechanism10.png/600px-CBS_mechanism10.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/CBS_mechanism10.png/800px-CBS_mechanism10.png 2x" data-file-width="1486" data-file-height="1265" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>Migration of the hydride from borane to the electrophilic ketone center occurs via a 6-membered ring transition state, leading to a four-membered ring intermediate ultimately providing the chiral product and regeneration of the catalyst. The reaction has also been of great use to natural products chemists (scheme 11).<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The synthesis of dysidiolide by Corey and co-workers was achieved via an enantioselective CBS reduction using a borane-dimethylsulfide complex.</p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:CBS_total_synthesis11.png" class="image"><img alt="CBS total synthesis11.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/CBS_total_synthesis11.png/550px-CBS_total_synthesis11.png" width="550" height="217" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/CBS_total_synthesis11.png/825px-CBS_total_synthesis11.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/CBS_total_synthesis11.png/1100px-CBS_total_synthesis11.png 2x" data-file-width="1909" data-file-height="753" /></a></div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Corey-Fuchs_alkyne_synthesis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Corey-Fuchs alkyne synthesis (page does not exist)">Corey-Fuchs alkyne synthesis</a> is the synthesis of terminal <a href="/wiki/Alkyne" title="Alkyne">alkynes</a> through a one-carbon homologation of aldehydes using <a href="/wiki/Triphenylphosphine" title="Triphenylphosphine">triphenylphosphine</a> and carbon tetrabromide.<sup id="cite_ref-reactions_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reactions-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The mechanism is similar to that of the <a href="/wiki/Wittig_reaction" title="Wittig reaction">Wittig reaction</a> by the formation of a phosphorus <a href="/wiki/Ylide" title="Ylide">ylide</a> with triphenylphosphine and carbon tetrabromide. Reacting the phosphorus ylide with the aldehyde substrate yields a dibromoolefin.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Corey-fuch_reaction12.png" class="image"><img alt="Corey-fuch reaction12.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Corey-fuch_reaction12.png/550px-Corey-fuch_reaction12.png" width="550" height="129" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Corey-fuch_reaction12.png/825px-Corey-fuch_reaction12.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Corey-fuch_reaction12.png/1100px-Corey-fuch_reaction12.png 2x" data-file-width="1933" data-file-height="453" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>On treatment with two equivalents of <i>n</i>-buLi, lithium halogen exchange and deprotonation yields a lithium acetylide species that undergoes hydrolysis to yield the terminal alkyne product (scheme 12).<sup id="cite_ref-reactions_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reactions-32">[32]</a></sup> More recently, a one-pot synthesis using a modified procedure has been developed.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> This synthetic transformation has been proven successful in the total synthesis (+)-taylorione by W.J. Kerr and co-workers (scheme 13).<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Corey-fuch_total_synthesis13.png" class="image"><img alt="Corey-fuch total synthesis13.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Corey-fuch_total_synthesis13.png/550px-Corey-fuch_total_synthesis13.png" width="550" height="128" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Corey-fuch_total_synthesis13.png/825px-Corey-fuch_total_synthesis13.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Corey-fuch_total_synthesis13.png/1100px-Corey-fuch_total_synthesis13.png 2x" data-file-width="1884" data-file-height="440" /></a></div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Corey%E2%80%93Kim_oxidation" title="Corey–Kim oxidation">Corey–Kim oxidation</a> was a newly developed transformation for the conversion of alcohols into corresponding aldehydes and ketones.<sup id="cite_ref-reactions_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reactions-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> This process offers a less toxic alternative to chromium based oxidations with the use of <i>N</i>-chlorosuccinimidosulfonium chloride (NCS), dimethylsulfide (DMS), and <a href="/wiki/Triethylamine" title="Triethylamine">triethylamine</a> (TEA). The Corey-Kim reagent is formed in <i>situ</i> when NCS and DMS are reacted to form dimethylsuccinimidosulfonium chloride species (scheme 14).<sup id="cite_ref-reactions_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reactions-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Corey_kim_ox14.png" class="image"><img alt="Corey kim ox14.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Corey_kim_ox14.png/550px-Corey_kim_ox14.png" width="550" height="144" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Corey_kim_ox14.png/825px-Corey_kim_ox14.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/Corey_kim_ox14.png/1100px-Corey_kim_ox14.png 2x" data-file-width="2117" data-file-height="553" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>The alkoxy sulfonium salt is deprotonated at the alpha position with triethylamine to afford the oxidized product. The reaction accommodates a wide array of functional groups, but allylic and benzylic alcohols are typically transformed into allylic and benzylic chlorides. Its application in synthesis is based on the mild protocol conditions and functional and protecting group compatibility. In the total synthesis of ingenol, Kuwajima and co-workers exploited the Corey-Kim oxidation by selectively oxidizing the less hindered secondary alcohol(scheme 15).<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Corey_kim_ox_synthesis_example15.png" class="image"><img alt="Corey kim ox synthesis example15.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Corey_kim_ox_synthesis_example15.png/550px-Corey_kim_ox_synthesis_example15.png" width="550" height="200" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Corey_kim_ox_synthesis_example15.png/825px-Corey_kim_ox_synthesis_example15.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Corey_kim_ox_synthesis_example15.png/1100px-Corey_kim_ox_synthesis_example15.png 2x" data-file-width="1745" data-file-height="636" /></a></div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Corey-Winter_Olefination" class="mw-redirect" title="Corey-Winter Olefination">Corey-Winter olefination</a> is a stereospecific transformation of 1,2-diols to alkenes involving the diol substrate, thiocarbonyldiimidazole, and excess trialkylphosphite.<sup id="cite_ref-reactions_32-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reactions-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The mechanism has been narrowed down to two possible pathways, but the exact mechanism is unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Specifically, the reaction between the thionocarbonate and the trialkylphosphite proceeds either through the formation of a phosphorus ylide species or a carbenoid intermediate. Nonetheless, the reaction is stereospecific for most substrates unless the product would lead to an exceedingly strained structure. The formation of sterically hindered trans alkenes present in 7-membered rings was attempted by Corey and coworkers, but was unsuccessful even when employing this new synthetic methodology incurring enormous ring strain. More importantly, stereospecfic alkenes are present in several natural products as the method continues to be exploited to yield a series of complex substrates. Professor T.K.M Shing <i>et al</i> used the Corey-Winter olefination reaction to synthesize (+)-Boesenoxide (scheme 16).<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Corey-winter_olefination_example16.png" class="image" title="total synthesis example of corey winter olefination"><img alt="total synthesis example of corey winter olefination" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Corey-winter_olefination_example16.png/600px-Corey-winter_olefination_example16.png" width="600" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Corey-winter_olefination_example16.png/900px-Corey-winter_olefination_example16.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Corey-winter_olefination_example16.png/1200px-Corey-winter_olefination_example16.png 2x" data-file-width="2249" data-file-height="451" /></a></div>
</div>
<ul>
<li>CBS enantioselective <a href="/wiki/Diels%E2%80%93Alder_reaction" title="Diels–Alder reaction">Diels-Alder reaction</a> has been developed using a similar scaffold to the enantioselective CBS reduction.<sup id="cite_ref-enantioselective_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enantioselective-33">[33]</a></sup> After the development of this reaction the CBS reagent proved to be a very versatile reagent for a series of several powerful synthetic transformations. The use of a chiral Lewis acid such as the CBS catalyst includes a broad range of unsaturated <a href="/wiki/Enone" title="Enone">enones</a> substrates. The reaction likely proceeds via a highly organized 6-membered ring pre-transition state to deliver highly enantio-enriched products (scheme 17).<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Diels_alder_TS17.png" class="image" title="enantioslective diels-alder transition state"><img alt="enantioslective diels-alder transition state" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Diels_alder_TS17.png/400px-Diels_alder_TS17.png" width="400" height="304" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Diels_alder_TS17.png/600px-Diels_alder_TS17.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Diels_alder_TS17.png/800px-Diels_alder_TS17.png 2x" data-file-width="1269" data-file-height="965" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>This transition state likely occurs because of favorable pi-stacking with the phenyl substituent.<sup id="cite_ref-enantioselective_33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-enantioselective-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The enantioselectivity of the process is facilitated from the diene approaching the dienophile from the opposite face of the phenyl substituent. The Diels-Alder reaction is one of the most powerful transformations in synthetic chemistry. The synthesis of natural products using the Diels-Alder reaction as a transform has been applied especially to the formation of six-membered rings(scheme 18).<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Diels_alder_example_enantioselective18.png" class="image" title="enantioslective diels-alder in total synthesis"><img alt="enantioslective diels-alder in total synthesis" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Diels_alder_example_enantioselective18.png/600px-Diels_alder_example_enantioselective18.png" width="600" height="140" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Diels_alder_example_enantioselective18.png/900px-Diels_alder_example_enantioselective18.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Diels_alder_example_enantioselective18.png/1200px-Diels_alder_example_enantioselective18.png 2x" data-file-width="2180" data-file-height="510" /></a></div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/w/index.php?title=Corey-Nicolaou_macrolactonization&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Corey-Nicolaou macrolactonization (page does not exist)">Corey-Nicolaou macrolactonization</a> provides the first method for preparing medium to large sized <a href="/wiki/Lactone" title="Lactone">lactones</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-reactions_32-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reactions-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Previously, intermolecular outcompeted intramolecular lactonization even at low concentrations. One big advantage of this reaction is that it is performed under neutral conditions allowing the presence of acid and base-labile functional groups. To date rings of 7 to 48 members have been successfully synthesized using this method.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Corey-nicolaou_macrolactonization19.png" class="image" title="mechanism of Corey-Nicolaou macrolactonization"><img alt="mechanism of Corey-Nicolaou macrolactonization" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Corey-nicolaou_macrolactonization19.png/600px-Corey-nicolaou_macrolactonization19.png" width="600" height="294" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Corey-nicolaou_macrolactonization19.png/900px-Corey-nicolaou_macrolactonization19.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Corey-nicolaou_macrolactonization19.png/1200px-Corey-nicolaou_macrolactonization19.png 2x" data-file-width="2239" data-file-height="1097" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>The reaction occurs in the presence of 2,2'-dipyridyl disulfide and triphenylphosphine. The reaction is generally refluxed in a nonpolar solvent such as <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a>. The mechanism begins with formation of the 2-pyridinethiol ester (scheme 19). Proton-transfer provides a dipolar intermediate in which the alkoxide <a href="/wiki/Nucleophile" title="Nucleophile">nucleophile</a> attacks the electrophilic carbonyl center, providing a tetrahedral intermediate that yields the macrolactone product. One of the first examples of this protocol was applied to the total synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Zearalenone" title="Zearalenone">zearalenone</a> (scheme 20).<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Macrolactonization_example20.png" class="image" title="macrolactonization total synthesis example"><img alt="macrolactonization total synthesis example" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Macrolactonization_example20.png/600px-Macrolactonization_example20.png" width="600" height="107" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Macrolactonization_example20.png/900px-Macrolactonization_example20.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Macrolactonization_example20.png/1200px-Macrolactonization_example20.png 2x" data-file-width="2221" data-file-height="397" /></a></div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Johnson-Corey-Chaykovsky_reaction" class="mw-redirect" title="Johnson-Corey-Chaykovsky reaction">Johnson-Corey-Chaykovsky reaction</a> is useful for the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Epoxide" title="Epoxide">epoxides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cyclopropane" title="Cyclopropane">cyclopropanes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-reactions_32-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-reactions-32">[32]</a></sup> The reaction forms a sulfur ylide in situ that reacts with enones, ketones, aldehydes, and <a href="/wiki/Imine" title="Imine">imines</a> to form corresponding epoxides, cyclopropanes, and <a href="/wiki/Aziridine" title="Aziridine">aziridines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Two sulfur ylide variants have been employed that give different chemeoselective products (scheme 21).The dimethylsulfoxonium methylide provides epoxides from ketones, but yields the cyclopropanes when enones are employed. Dimethylsulfonium methylide transforms ketones and enones to the corresponding epoxides. Dimethylsulfonium methylide is much more reactive and less stable than dimethylsulfoxonium methylide, so it is generated at low temperatures.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Corey-chakovsky_reaction21.png" class="image" title="corey-chaykovsky selectivity"><img alt="corey-chaykovsky selectivity" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Corey-chakovsky_reaction21.png/600px-Corey-chakovsky_reaction21.png" width="600" height="372" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Corey-chakovsky_reaction21.png/900px-Corey-chakovsky_reaction21.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Corey-chakovsky_reaction21.png/1200px-Corey-chakovsky_reaction21.png 2x" data-file-width="2242" data-file-height="1391" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>Based on their reactivity, another distinct advantage of these two variants is that kinetically they provide a difference in diastereoselectivity. The reaction is very well established, and enantioselective variants (catalytic and stoichiometric) have also been achieved. From a retrosynthetic analysis standpoint, this reaction provides a reasonable alternative to conventional epoxidation reactions with alkenes (scheme 22). Danishefsky utilized this methodology for the synthesis of taxol. Diastereoselectivity is established by 1,3 interactions in the transition state required for epoxide closure.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Corey-chakovsky_example22.png" class="image" title="corey-chaykovsky total synthesis example"><img alt="corey-chaykovsky total synthesis example" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Corey-chakovsky_example22.png/600px-Corey-chakovsky_example22.png" width="600" height="145" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Corey-chakovsky_example22.png/900px-Corey-chakovsky_example22.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Corey-chakovsky_example22.png/1200px-Corey-chakovsky_example22.png 2x" data-file-width="1820" data-file-height="439" /></a></div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Total_syntheses">Total syntheses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elias_James_Corey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Total syntheses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>E. J. Corey and his research group have completed many <a href="/wiki/Total_synthesis" title="Total synthesis">total syntheses</a>. At least 265 molecules have been synthesized in the Corey group since 1950.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>His 1969 total syntheses of several <a href="/wiki/Prostaglandin" title="Prostaglandin">prostaglandins</a> are considered classics.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Specifically the synthesis of Prostaglandin F<sub>2a</sub> presents several challenges. The presence of both cis and trans olefins as well as five asymmetric carbon atoms renders the molecule a desirable topological challenge for organic chemist. Corey's retrosynthetic analysis outlines a few key disconnections that lead to simplified precursors (scheme 23).</p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Prostaglandin_retro23.png" class="image"><img alt="Prostaglandin retro23.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Prostaglandin_retro23.png/600px-Prostaglandin_retro23.png" width="600" height="327" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Prostaglandin_retro23.png/900px-Prostaglandin_retro23.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Prostaglandin_retro23.png/1200px-Prostaglandin_retro23.png 2x" data-file-width="2251" data-file-height="1226" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>Molecular simplification began first by disconnecting both carbon chains with a Wittig reaction and Horner-Wadsworth Emmons modification. The Wittig reaction affords the cis product, while the Horner-Wadsworth Emmons produces the trans olefin. The published synthesis reveals a 1:1 diastereomeric mixture of the carbonyl reduction using zinc borohydride. However, years later Corey and co-workers established the CBS reduction. One of the examples that exemplified this protocol was an intermediate in the prostaglandin synthesis revealing a 9:1 mixture of the desired diastereomer (scheme 24).<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="center">
<div class="floatnone"><a href="/wiki/File:Prostoglandin_CBS24.png" class="image"><img alt="Prostoglandin CBS24.png" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Prostoglandin_CBS24.png/500px-Prostoglandin_CBS24.png" width="500" height="245" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Prostoglandin_CBS24.png/750px-Prostoglandin_CBS24.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Prostoglandin_CBS24.png/1000px-Prostoglandin_CBS24.png 2x" data-file-width="1934" data-file-height="948" /></a></div>
</div>
<p>The iodolactonization transform affords an allylic alcohol leading to a key Baeyer-Villiger intermediate. This oxidation regioselectively inserts an oxygen atom between the ketone and the most electron-rich site. The pivotal intermediate leads to a straightforward conversion to the Diels-Alder structural goal, which provides the carbon framework for the functionalized cyclopentane ring. Later Corey developed an asymmetric Diels-Alder reaction employing a chiral oxazoborolidine, greatly simplifying the synthetic route to the prostaglandins.</p>
<p>Other notable syntheses:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Longifolene" title="Longifolene">Longifolene</a><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ginkgolide" title="Ginkgolide">Ginkgolides</a> A<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> and B<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lactacystin" title="Lactacystin">Lactacystin</a><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Miroestrol" title="Miroestrol">Miroestrol</a><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ecteinascidin_743" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecteinascidin 743">Ecteinascidin 743</a><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Salinosporamide_A" title="Salinosporamide A">Salinosporamide A</a><sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Publications">Publications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elias_James_Corey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Publications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>E.J. Corey has more than 1100 publications.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> In 2002, the American Chemical Society (ACS) recognized him as the "Most Cited Author in Chemistry". In 2007, he received the first ACS Publications Division "Cycle of Excellence High Impact Contributor Award"<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> and was ranked the number one chemist in terms of research impact by the Hirsch Index (<a href="/wiki/H-index" title="H-index">h-index</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> His books include:</p>
<ul>
<li>E.J. Corey and Laszlo Kurti, <i>Enantioselective Chemical Synthesis: Methods, Logic, and Practice</i>, Direct Book Publishing LLC, 2010, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-615-39515-9" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-615-39515-9">978-0-615-39515-9</a></li>
<li>Elias James Corey, Xue-Min Cheng. <i>The Logic of Chemical Synthesis</i>. Wiley-Interscience, 1995, <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-471-11594-0" title="Special:BookSources/0-471-11594-0">0-471-11594-0</a>.</li>
<li>E. J. Corey, Barbara Czako, Laszlo Kurti. <i>Molecules and Medicine</i> John Wiley &amp; Sons, 2008.</li>
<li>Name reactions in heterocyclic chemistry / edited by Jie-Jack Li&#160;; scientific editor, E.J. Corey. Hoboken, N.J.&#160;: Wiley-Interscience, 2005.</li>
<li>Name reactions for functional group transformations / edited by Jie Jack Li, E.J. Corey. Hoboken, N.J.&#160;: Wiley-Interscience, 2007.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Altom_suicide">Altom suicide</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elias_James_Corey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Altom suicide">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Jason_Altom" title="Jason Altom">Jason Altom</a></div>
<p>Among the hundreds of graduate-students supervised by Prof. Corey was <a href="/wiki/Jason_Altom" title="Jason Altom">Jason Altom</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-chronicle_suicide_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chronicle_suicide-72">[72]</a></sup> Altom's suicide caused controversy because he explicitly blamed Corey, his research advisor, for his suicide.<sup id="cite_ref-NYTAltom_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTAltom-73">[73]</a></sup> Corey was devastated and bewildered by Altom's death.<sup id="cite_ref-Globe_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Globe-74">[74]</a></sup> Altom cited in his 1998 farewell note "abusive research supervisors" as one reason for taking his life. Altom's suicide note also contained explicit instructions on how to reform the relationship between students and their supervisors. Corey is quoted as stating: "[T]hat letter doesn't make sense. At the end, Jason must have been delusional or irrational in the extreme." Corey also is on record as stating that he never questioned Mr. Altom's intellectual contributions. "I did my best to guide Jason as a mountain guide would to guide someone climbing a mountain. I did my best every step of the way," Corey states. "My conscience is clear. Everything Jason did came out of our partnership. We never had the slightest disagreement.".<sup id="cite_ref-chronicle_suicide_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chronicle_suicide-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a result of Altom's death, the Department of Chemistry accepted a proposal allowing graduate students to ask two additional faculty members to play a small advisory role in preparing a thesis.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYTAltom_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYTAltom-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Foundation_for_Suicide_Prevention" title="American Foundation for Suicide Prevention">American Foundation for Suicide Prevention</a> (AFSP) cited the <i><a href="/wiki/New_York_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Times">New York Times</a></i> article on Altom's suicide as an example of problematic reporting,<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> and suggested that Corey was unfairly scapegoated.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Boston_Globe" title="The Boston Globe">The Boston Globe</a></i>, Altom's suicide note indicated fear that his career hopes were doomed, but <i>The Globe</i> also cited students and professors as saying that Altom actually retained Corey's support.<sup id="cite_ref-Globe_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Globe-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Corey_Group_members">Corey Group members</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elias_James_Corey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Corey Group members">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As of 2010, approximately 700 people have been Corey Group members. A database of 580 former members and their current affiliation was developed for Corey's 80th birthday in July, 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Woodward–Hoffmann_rules"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Woodward.E2.80.93Hoffmann_rules">Woodward–Hoffmann rules</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elias_James_Corey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Woodward–Hoffmann rules">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>When awarded the Priestley Medal in 2004, E. J. Corey created a controversy with his claim to have inspired <a href="/wiki/Robert_Burns_Woodward" title="Robert Burns Woodward">Robert Burns Woodward</a> prior to the development of the <a href="/wiki/Woodward%E2%80%93Hoffmann_rules" title="Woodward–Hoffmann rules">Woodward–Hoffmann rules</a>. Corey wrote:</p>
<p><i>"On May 4, 1964, I suggested to my colleague R. B. Woodward a simple explanation involving the symmetry of the perturbed (HOMO) molecular orbitals for the stereoselective cyclobutene → 1,3-butadiene and 1,3,5-hexatriene → cyclohexadiene conversions that provided the basis for the further development of these ideas into what became known as the Woodward–Hoffmann rules."</i><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>This was Corey's first public statement on his claim that starting on May 5, 1964 Woodward put forth Corey's explanation as his own thought with no mention of Corey and the conversation of May 4. Corey had discussed his claim privately with Hoffmann and close colleagues since 1964. Corey mentions that he made the Priestley statement <i>"so the historical record would be correct"</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>Corey's claim and contribution were publicly rebutted by <a href="/wiki/Roald_Hoffmann" title="Roald Hoffmann">Roald Hoffmann</a> in the journal <a href="/wiki/Angewandte_Chemie" title="Angewandte Chemie">Angewandte Chemie</a>. In the rebuttal, Hoffmann states that he asked Corey over the course of their long discussion of the matter why Corey did not make the issue public. Corey responded that he thought such a public disagreement would hurt Harvard and that he would not "consider doing anything against Harvard, to which I was and am so devoted." Corey also hoped that Woodward himself would correct the historical record "as he grew older, more considerate, and more sensitive to his own conscience."<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Woodward died suddenly of a heart attack in his sleep in 1979.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honors">Awards and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elias_James_Corey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Awards and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>E.J. Corey has received more than 40 major awards including the <a href="/wiki/Linus_Pauling_Award" title="Linus Pauling Award">Linus Pauling Award</a> (1973), <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Medal" title="Franklin Medal">Franklin Medal</a> (1978), <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedron_Prize" title="Tetrahedron Prize">Tetrahedron Prize</a> (1983), <a href="/wiki/Wolf_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Wolf Prize in Chemistry">Wolf Prize in Chemistry</a> (1986), <a href="/wiki/National_Medal_of_Science" title="National Medal of Science">National Medal of Science</a> (1988), <a href="/wiki/Japan_Prize" title="Japan Prize">Japan Prize</a> (1989), <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> (1990), Roger Adams Award (1993), and the <a href="/wiki/Priestley_Medal" title="Priestley Medal">Priestley Medal</a> (2004).<sup id="cite_ref-major-awards_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-major-awards-13">[13]</a></sup> He was inducted into the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Chi_Sigma" title="Alpha Chi Sigma">Alpha Chi Sigma</a> Hall of Fame in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-alphachisigma.org_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alphachisigma.org-9">[9]</a></sup> As of 2008, he has been awarded 19 honorary degrees from universities around the world including <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a> (UK), <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge University</a> (UK), and <a href="/wiki/National_Chung_Cheng_University" title="National Chung Cheng University">National Chung Cheng University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> In 2013, the E.J. Corey Institute of Biomedical Research (CIBR) opened in Jiangyin, Jiangsu Province, China.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>Corey was elected a <a href="/wiki/List_of_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_elected_in_1998" title="List of Fellows of the Royal Society elected in 1998">Foreign Member of the Royal Society (ForMemRS) in 1998</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-formemrs_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-formemrs-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Elias_James_Corey&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li><span style="font-size:90%;">2009</span> <a href="/wiki/Venkatraman_Ramakrishnan" title="Venkatraman Ramakrishnan">Venkatraman Ramakrishnan</a> / <a href="/wiki/Thomas_A._Steitz" title="Thomas A. Steitz">Thomas A. Steitz</a> / <a href="/wiki/Ada_Yonath" title="Ada Yonath">Ada E. Yonath</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">2010</span> <a href="/wiki/Richard_F._Heck" title="Richard F. Heck">Richard F. Heck</a> / <a href="/wiki/Akira_Suzuki_(chemist)" title="Akira Suzuki (chemist)">Akira Suzuki</a> / <a href="/wiki/Ei-ichi_Negishi" title="Ei-ichi Negishi">Ei-ichi Negishi</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">2011</span> <a href="/wiki/Dan_Shechtman" title="Dan Shechtman">Dan Shechtman</a></li>

<li><span style="font-size:90%;">2013</span> <a href="/wiki/Martin_Karplus" title="Martin Karplus">Martin Karplus</a> / <a href="/wiki/Michael_Levitt" title="Michael Levitt">Michael Levitt</a> / <a href="/wiki/Arieh_Warshel" title="Arieh Warshel">Arieh Warshel</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">2014</span> <a href="/wiki/Eric_Betzig" title="Eric Betzig">Eric Betzig</a> / <a href="/wiki/Stefan_Hell" title="Stefan Hell">Stefan Hell</a> / <a href="/wiki/William_E._Moerner" title="William E. Moerner">William E. Moerner</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">2015</span> <a href="/wiki/Tomas_Lindahl" title="Tomas Lindahl">Tomas Lindahl</a> / <a href="/wiki/Paul_L._Modrich" title="Paul L. Modrich">Paul L. Modrich</a> / <a href="/wiki/Aziz_Sancar" title="Aziz Sancar">Aziz Sancar</a></li>
<li><span style="font-size:90%;">2016</span> <a href="/wiki/Jean-Pierre_Sauvage" title="Jean-Pierre Sauvage">Jean-Pierre Sauvage</a> / <a href="/wiki/Fraser_Stoddart" title="Fraser Stoddart">Fraser Stoddart</a> / <a href="/wiki/Ben_Feringa" title="Ben Feringa">Ben Feringa</a></li>

<th scope="row" class="navbox-group" style="width:1%">1980s</th>
